  • 93. User defined macro '〔latex …​' is not defined. Did you mean '@date'? at /Users/verhasp/github/javax0.github.io/_posts/2023/2023-03-01-inplace-rearrange.adoc.jam/174:35

title: Rearrange an array in place layout: posta comments: on --- {%@import javax0.jim%} {%@comment

%} {%@sep 〔〕%} = Rearrange and array in place 〔useLatex〕

This article is about an algorithmic problem. I did not find a solution to this problem either thinking about it, searching the net or talking to friends. The problem itself is simple. At the first reading may lure the reader into false sensing of simplicity. You may feel that this should not be an issue. But it is. At least for me.

Nothing Java. Nothing programming other than it is about an algorithmic problem.

〔section Problem Statement〕

There is an array of 〔latex 2n〕 elements. The array is 〔latex A=\{a_1,a_2, …​ , a_n, b_1, b_2, …​ , b_b\}〕. What steps should be taken to rearrange the array so that 〔latex A=\{a_1,b_1,a_2,b_2, …​ , a_n,b_n\}〕 with limited, maximum 〔latex O(1)〕 memory, and 〔latex O(n)〕 time.

〔section ChatGPT solution〕

Sine generative AI is sweeping the media and the internet, I thought that I should try to use it to solve the problem. After all, if it will take my job just in a few years, at least we can let it do some work for us till then.

Here is the answer, no surprise: totally wrong:

Warning
Wrong answer from ChapGPT

To rearrange the array 〔latex A=\{a_1,a_2, …​ , a_n, b_1, b_2, …​ , b_n\}〕 into 〔latex A=\{a_1,b_1,a_2,b_2, …​ , a_n,b_n\}〕 with limited, maximum O(1) memory, and 〔latex O(n)〕 time you can follow the steps below:

Traverse the array and for each element at index 〔latex i〕, swap it with the element at index 〔latex i+n〕.

For i=1 to n:
  Swap A[i] with A[i+n]

After the traversal is complete, the array will be rearranged as 〔latex A=\{a_1,b_1,a_2,b_2, …​ , a_n,b_n\}〕. The above algorithm has a time complexity of O(n) and uses only O(1) memory as only a constant number of variables are used for swapping elements in the array.

After following these steps, the original array 〔latex A=\{a_1,a_2, …​ , a_n, b_1, b_2, …​ , b_n\}〕 would be rearranged as 〔latex A=\{a_1,b_1,a_2,b_2, …​ , a_n,b_n\}〕.

The described algorithm is 〔latex O(n)〕 time and 〔latex O(1)〕 memory, but the resulting array will be 〔latex A=\{b_1,b_2, …​ , b_n,a_1,a_2, …​ , a_n\}〕.

Note
 I am not worried about being replaced by an AI as a software engineer. Yet.

〔section Without the 〔latex| O(1)〕memory constraint〕

The problem is not that hard if you do not have the 〔latex O(1)〕 memory constraint. As a matter of fact it is so easy that it can be solved even by ChapGPT. Or can it?

Note
Simpler problem, still not good answer from ChapGPT

To rearrange the array 〔latex A={a_1,a_2, …​ , a_n, b_1, b_2, …​ , b_n}〕 into 〔latex A={a_1,b_1,a_2,b_2, …​ , a_n,b_n}〕, you can follow the steps below:

Create two new arrays, one for the 〔latex a〕 values and another for the 〔latex b〕 values. Initialize an empty array for the 〔latex a〕 values, say 〔latex a_{new}〕. Initialize an empty array for the 〔latex b〕 values, say 〔latex b_{new}〕. Traverse the original array and separate the 〔latex a〕 and 〔latex b〕 values into two separate arrays.

For i=1 to n:
Add 〔latex a_i〕 to 〔latex a_{new}〕
Add 〔latex b_i〕 to 〔latex b_{new}〕

For i=n+1 to 2n:
Add 〔latex b_i〕 to 〔latex a_{new}〕
Add 〔latex a_i〕 to 〔latex b_{new}〕

Concatenate the two new arrays in the desired order to get the final result. Concatenate 〔latex a_{new}〕 and 〔latex b_{new}〕 in the order 〔latex a_{new}, b_{new}〕 to get the desired result. After following these steps, the original array 〔latex A={a_1,a_2, …​ , a_n, b_1, b_2, …​ , b_n}〕 would be rearranged as 〔latex A={a_1,b_1,a_2,b_2, …​ , a_n,b_n}〕.

I believe there is no need to explain why the solution is wrong, and overly complicated. The allocation of the two arrays is unnecessarily complicated. We need only one extra array of length 〔latex 2n〕, say 〔latex T〕. Then the code will look:

For i=1 to n:
Add 〔latex a_i〕 to 〔latex T〕
Add 〔latex b_i〕 to 〔latex T〕

Voilà, you have the result in 〔latex T〕.

Note
 Still not worried.

〔section Without the 〔latex| O(n)〕 time constraint: 〔latex O(n^2)〕〕

This problem is also simple to solve, but for some reason ChaptGPT gave such a foolish answer that I decided not to copy here. The solution is

  1. to take 〔latex b_i〕out from the array for every 〔latex i=1,n〕one by one,

  2. push every element one position higher below 〔latex b_i〕 until we get to the place where 〔latex b_i〕 will get

  3. put 〔latex b_i〕 to its place.

〔png :insert-rearrange:width=50%, align=center〕

For i=1 to n:
Store 〔latex b_i〕 being at the position 〔latex| n+i〕to a variable named 〔latex x〕
Move all objects from 〔latex| 2i〕 till 〔latex n+i〕 to the next position
Put 〔latex b_i〕from the variable 〔latex x〕to the position 2i

During the first step we have to move 〔latex n-1〕 objects. During the second step we have to move 〔latex n-2〕 objects, and so on. Moving all the 〔latex b_i; i=1,n〕elements will need 〔latex \sum_{i=1}^{n-1} (n-i) = \frac{n(n-1)}{2}〕 moves. It means that this algorithm is 〔latex O(n^2)〕 time.

〔section Without the 〔latex| O(n)〕 time constraint: 〔latex O(n \log n)〕〕

There is a more complex solution that is 〔latex O(n \log n)〕 time.

If the array length is 2, a.k.a. the array is 〔latex A=\{a_1,b_1\}〕, then the solution is trivial. In that case do not need to do anything, just return the array as it is.

Funny (wrong) suggestion from CoPilot

If the array is longer, then we can split it into two parts, rearrange them separately, and then merge the results.

It is fun to write articles with CoPilot switched on (pronounce it as Copy-a-Lot). I do not say it is easier, or less work. Rather the contrary, but undeniably more fun. Cursive word in the above paragraphs all were suggested by CoPilot.

Assume that the array length is 〔latex n=2^m〕. Divide the array into four parts:

  • 〔latex Q_1=[a_1-a_\frac n2]〕,

  • 〔latex Q_2=[a_{\frac n2+1},a_n]〕,

  • 〔latex Q_3=[b_1-b_\frac n2]〕,

  • 〔latex Q_4=[b_{\frac n2+1},b_n]〕,

Swap the elements of 〔latex Q2〕 and 〔latex Q3〕in a single loop:

For i=1 to 〔latex| \frac n2〕:
Swap 〔latex a_{\frac n2+i}〕 and 〔latex b_i〕

Essentially we transform the array 〔latex Q_1|Q_2|Q_3|Q_4〕 into 〔latex Q_1|Q_3|Q_2|Q_4〕. After this, the algorithm can be applied for the sub arrays 〔latex Q_1|Q_3〕 and 〔latex Q_1|Q_4〕.

The assumption that 〔latex n=2^m〕helps to halve the array down to the length 2.

The algorithm running time 〔latex T(n) = 2T(\frac n2) + O(n)〕. The 〔latex O(n)〕term is the time needed to swap the elements of 〔latex Q_2〕and 〔latex Q_3〕. The resulting time complexity is 〔latex O(n \log n)〕.
